<B>INFORMATION TO USERS</B>
This material was produced from a microfilm copy of the original document. While the most advanced technological means to photograph and reproduce this document have been used, the quality is heavily dependent upon the quality of the original submitted.
The following explanation of techniques is provided to help you understand markings or patterns which may appear on this reproduction.
	1. The sign or "target" for pages apparently lacking from the document photographed is "Missing Page(s)". If it was possible to obtain the missing page(s) or section, they are spliced into the film along with adjacent pages. This may have necessitated cutting thru an image and duplicating adjacent pages to insure you complete continuity.
	2. When an image on the film is obliterated with a large round black mark, it is an indication that the photographer suspected that the copy may have moved during exposure and thus cause a blurred image. You will find a good image of the page in the adjacent frame.
	3. When a map, drawing or chart, etc., was part of the material being photographed the photographer followed a definite method in "sectioning" the material. It is customary to begin photoing at the upper left hand corner of a large sheet and to continue photoing from left to right in equal sections with a small overlap. If necessary, sectioning is continued again &ndash; beginning below the first row and continuing on until complete.
	4. The majority of users indicate that the textual content is of greatest value, however, a somewhat higher quality reproduction could be made from "photographs" if essential to the understanding of the dissertation. Silver prints of "photographs" may be ordered at additional charge by writing the Order Department, giving the catalog number, title, author and specific pages you wish reproduced.
	5. PLEASE NOTE: Some pages may have indistinct print. Filmed as received.

INTRODUCTION
	An Index of Words to Eutropius is certainly an indispensable addition to scholarship, to the advanced study of Eutropius, and to library equipment. The requirements for implements of study which can be depended on for accuracy in detail as well as in broad research have created a demand for lexicons and indexes to individual authors, and this situation is being met by the past and present preparation of such works as Wetmore's Index to Vergil, Jenkins' Index to Terence, Lodge's Lexicon to Plautus and by the various Lexicons to Caesar, Petronius, Tacitus, Sophocles and others. An Index to Eutropius has not yet, to the best of my knowledge, been presented. I have undertaken this presentation, therefore, with the conviction that the <U>Breviarium Historiae Romanae</U> is of sufficient importance to warrant much further study, especially as references to it are appearing in books for the earlier years in the study of Latin. Students of philology, of language and of history need these indexes to assist them to reach quickly and accurately certain words and usages and events without being compelled to possess a complete acquaintance with every text necessary to their work.
	The present Index follows essentially the method used by the compilers of the Indexes to Vergil, Seneca, and Suetonius<SUP>1</SUP>. The text of Eutropius employed is the latest edition(1919) of the Teubner series, number 1346, edited and established by F. Ruehl. The use of each word in the text has been carefully considered, noted, and checked so that the result when tabulated gives the actual form of each occurrence and the exact location in the text of the appearance of that form. Certain necessary but simple abbreviations as to form, use, and textual variation have been added in order to leave no doubt as to any detail. The order of forms follows that in Allen and Greenough's New Latin Grammar with passive forms following those of the active voice; the adjective is to be read across; the vocative and locative cases follow the ablative. Undesig-nated forms may be identified by the spelling or by their position in the sequence. Simple numbers appear in their alphabetical order while compounds and the Roman numerals appear on the last pages(97-100) of the Index. In the identification of forms the Roman numeral refers to the Book, the following Arabic numerals to the chapters.
Where Ruehl has noted difficulties in the text I have compared them with the texts of Dietsch, of Droysen,

<B>APPENDIX</B>
	Many of the studies on Eutropius already published concern the establishment of the text and the validity of the author's statements. Individual writers of antiquity must first be studied in regard to textual criticism with the view of establishing as definitely as possible a correct reading of the literary production; without such an evaluation the veracity of the text would hardly permit of a quotable authority. The chief sources of Eutropius in literature are extant and cover a major portion of the times treated by him, hence it has been possible to verify many important features and to present a taxt of reasonable finality.
	Eutropius and Sextus Rufus, contemporaries, appear to have written their Breviaria by command of the Emperor Valens since the former states in his dedication and preface that he writes "ex voluntate mansuetudinis tuae" and the latter claims "parebo libens praeceptis", but in considering the gratuitous terms addressed to Eastern rulers such as Valens it may be that the Emperor made some remark as to the convenience of having a quicker method of learning Roman history than to spend days on the original sources. More probably, however, Valens had been speculating on how the glory of his reign compared with that of past rulers<SUP>1</SUP>. Quick to sense the possibilities of gaining favor by the preparation of an abbreviated and simplified story of Roman history the learned professors must have competed to produce something short and easy for an Emperor whom Ammianus Marcelinus characterized as "subagreste ingenium, nullis vetustatis lectionibus expolitum".<SUP>2</SUP> The need of a new school-book could also be met in this manner by royal favor. Eutropius and Rufus, being professors of history<SUP>3</SUP> or at least proficient in rhetoric<SUP>4</SUP> and the knowledge of earlier days were moved to produce their breviaria with appropriate dedications in deference to his "suggestion". That of Eutropius appeared first and was written in chronological order while that of Rufus, which treats of the various divisions of the empire, must have followed. Eutropius says of the expedition under Julian of which he was a member that the Emperor "ingenti apparatu. . . aliquot oppida et castella Persarum in deditionem accepit vel vi expugnavit"<SUP>5</SUP> and Rufus leans so much upon the authority of Eutropius at this point that he writes "Is cum ingenti apparatu. . .multa Persarum oppida et castella aut suscepit dedita, aut manu cepit".<SUP>6</SUP> Since Eutropius says "cui

expeditioni ego quoque interfui"<SUP>1</SUP> and since Rufus makes no mention of accompanying the army the former should be the authority.
	The name Eutropius has its manuscript and testimonial authority but to call him Flavius Eutropius as do Sigonius and the Classical Dictionaries of Harper and of Smith is scarcely permissible. The single name Eutropius is twice mentioned in the Fasti as consuls for the years 387 and 399. Flavius was a popular name in the Fasti at that time since the consuls for 384 were Fl.Ricomer and Fl. Clearchus while for 389 they were Fl. Timasius and Fl. Promotus with at least a half of the remainder at that period being listed also as Fl., but the identification of our author with either consular Eutropius or with any other derived from testimonials, history or inscriptions is difficult or a matter of conjecture as Pirogoff has demonstrated in his discussion of possibilities.<SUP>2</SUP> Reference to Eutropius is contained in a recently edited work written by Sicco Polenton which makes the following addition that Eutropius "Ad litteras vero, in quibus assidue et quantum posset ex negocio versaretur,librum de grammatica fecit.Ad historiam autem jam senex factus edidit libros decem."<SUP>3</SUP> This may
***
1. X 16.
2. Pirogoff,W., op.cit.,p. 5ff.; cf. also article on Eutropius In Pauly-Wissowa.
3. Sicconis Polentonis Scriptorum Illustrium Libri (edited B.L.Ullman), American Academy in Rome, 1928.
***
link Eutropius to him who was "proconsul Asiae"<SUP>1</SUP>. Neither is it possible to designate him as a Christian, because he treats Julian with the same disinterested impartiality as Constantine. But if it is conceded that Christian writers of that century were prone to follow a system of vigorous vituperation against eminent pagans and to betray their religious inclinations by that method, then our Eutropius was a broad-minded pagan.
	As to the Latinity of Eutropius it may be stated that he wrote simply and easily although he lacked a strictly classical Latin; his style, however, is brief and sufficiently in accord with classical grammar to be used as a school-book and to furnish references.<SUP>2</SUP> It is true that he employs a few words which are late and in the use of which the Dictionary quotes Eutropius alone.<SUP>3</SUP> Such words occur only once or twice and do not interfere with the generally classical vocabulary. He was borrowing obviously from previously written sources, and, from passages comparing him with the originals, he appears to have made use of their language and idiom (although so far as I have studied I find small indication of definite usages which would stamp his writings as containing archaisms with respect to his period). Some words and phrases, according
***
1. Amm. Marc. 29 i 36.
2. White,J.T., Latin-English Dictionary for junior students.
3. Cf. dubietas,medietas,adfectator in White,op.cit.
***
to the Index, do show a tendency to localization as if they were favorites of the source he used at that point.<SUP>1</SUP> The fact that "plebs" occurs once only, that "populus" does not occur after the reign of Tiberius, and that "Romani" for the people as a whole occurs infrequently after the same period indicates the historical development of individuals and the decline of the originally prominent social state.
The occurrences of "morior" and "obeo" illustrate a change in the use of words since the former shows fifteen of its nineteen forms before Book VIII and the latter thirteen of its seventeen forms after Book VII. Another feature of Eutropius is his use of numbers. Perhaps this situation is due to his sources but it is apparent that he knew more about numbers of men, of towns, and of animals hundreds of years before his time than he did about events concerned with numbers near and in his own day. He knows that Cincinnatus accomplished his work as Dictator in 20 days, that 8,000 Tuscan captives followed the triumph of C. Marcius, that Paulus captured 70 cities of Epirus, that L.Ventidius Bassus overcame the Persians in three battles, yet he can not, or does not state how many captives Maximian threw to the lions, nor in how many battles Constantine overthrew Maxentius, nor with how many men Julian went against the Persians nor how many towns he captured. "Ingens", "multus", and "aliquot", in comparison with the period of time covered, are more often used toward the close of the work to escape definite numbers.
***
1. "Nonnullus" and "idoneus" in Books IX and X; "quod"(conj.) most frequently in Book II.
***
Numbers, in fact, except those used of years of Rome, of personal age or of reign have infrequent appearance after Book VI. Whether this is due to a similar deficiency in the source material or to a dislike of causing unfavorable comparison would be a profitable study.
	This in turn brings up the question of the scope itself of the breviarium. At the time when breviaria were so copiously produced the involved Latinity of the classical writers was undoubtedly a hindrance to the majority of the readers and previous records had to be reduced to contemporary vocabulary and to a simpler style especially for the eastern portion of the empire where Greek was predominant.<SUP>1</SUP>
In one Preface<SUP>2</SUP> I notice that Eutropius is considered to have attempted to introduce to a more corrupt East some of the solid virtues of an early western culture and this may have been the motive for including such stories as those about Regulus, Pyrrhus, and Caesar at the death of Pompey. Since the text has been almost definitely established and much written on sources, it seems that more should be done concerning the subjects, personnages, and material which those who prepared breviaria saw fit to include; suggestions as to the spirit of the times which might have been responsible both for the breviaria and for the selections for the readers; there is also a base for a study of what buildings are mentioned and what references there are to literary men and
***
1. Translations of Eutropius were made shortly afterward by Paianius and Capito into Greek, but they seem to have dubious value for reconstruction except as to dates, numbers or names.
2. To the edition by Tzschucke(see Bibliography) p. xvii.
***
literature. The great vogue for lists of omens and portents (cf. Julius Obsequens) has not made itself felt in Eutropius and the gossip and intrigues of the courts so prominent in biographies of preceding centuries are in general missing from the works of both Eutropius and Rufus. With a few exceptions, women figure but minutely in this Breviarium and sons are the only younger persons who receive notice. Wars and military successes bulk large; reverses receive even more space than the influence of certain noble women. Probably this apportionment was congruent with the eastern conception of important facts. Then it may be considered how Eutropius, even in epitomizing, could remark on the joy of freeing Rome from the rule of the kings, say that an event took place while Rome was free, designate as "exsecrandum et lacrimabile" the civil war by which the founder of the Julian house came to power, and finally rejoice that noble men and the descendants of liberators removed this tyrant who was acting insolently against the custom of Roman freedom. His treatment of later Emperors appears impartial as he considers some noble, some obscure and worthless, condemning Jovian as reprehensible for his shameful peace with the Persians although he admits that he was "vir alias neque iners neque inprudens", but in the case of the present rulers, Valentinian and Valens, discretion at last comes to the fore by the flattering way in which he desires a more capable pen for the delineation of contemporary affairs.<SUP>1</SUP>
	Eutropius, therefore, presents material for several studies along the lines noted above both with reference to subjects which may be investigated without the help of the Index Verborum and especially those in which the Index is indispensable. Such studies, although they may be on the 'minutiae' of an author are definite contributions to certain fields and should be pursued.
***
1. "Nam reliqua stilo maiore dicenda sunt. Quae nunc non tam praetermittimus, quam ad maiorem scribendi diligentiam reservamus" X 18. In this he is followed by Rufus in a similar valedictory: "Quam magno deinceps ore tua, 0 princeps invicte, facta incluta sunt personanda? quibus me, licet imparem dicendi nisu, et aevo gravior, praeparabo: . . ." c.XXIX.
